Method of Learning
beginner
8 min
The Three Primary Sources of Knowledge: Sense, Reason, and Revelation
Author defines and argues for a tripartite classification of knowledge sources.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three primary sources of knowledge according to the author and justify each using the Quranic evidence from Surah an-Nahl 16:78.

Core Idea
The Quran gives us three avenues: hearing, sight, and heart – corresponding to transmitted, empirical, and rational knowledge.

This passage grounds an Islamic epistemology directly in the Qur'an. The author takes a researcher’s inference from verse 16:78 to establish a clear tripartite classification: auditory (sam'iyya) knowledge includes transmitted sciences, visual (bassariyya) knowledge includes empirical sciences, and heart-based (fu'adiyya) knowledge includes rational and spiritual knowledge. This lesson will walk you through the verse, the classification, and how it organizes Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Locate the Quranic verse
Read the verse: 'And Allah brought you out from the wombs of your mothers while you knew nothing, and made for you hearing, sight, and hearts that perhaps you would be grateful' (16:78). Identify the three faculties mentioned.
The verse uses al-sam' (hearing), al-absar (sight), and al-af'idah (hearts).
2
Extract the three knowledge types
The author follows a researcher’s conclusion: hearing corresponds to 'auditory knowledge' (sam'iyya) including transmitted sciences; sight corresponds to 'visual knowledge' (bassariyya) including empirical sciences; the heart corresponds to 'heart knowledge' (fu'adiyya) including pure rational knowledge.
Note that 'heart' here is the seat of intellect, not just feeling.
3
Map each type to an Islamic science
Assign one traditional Islamic discipline to each category: Fiqh (transmitted via hearing/reading), Medicine (empirical, based on sight/observation), Kalam (rational, based on intellectual contemplation).
The author explicitly includes 'transmitted sciences' and 'empirical sciences' in auditory and visual categories, respectively.
4
Distinguish primary from secondary sources
The author lists sense, reason, and infallible report (revelation) as primary. Inspiration and true dreams are secondary. This hierarchy is derived from the verse’s primary listing: hearing, sight, hearts.
Revelation is an infallible report; it subsumes auditory knowledge of religious texts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الباب الثالث - مقدمة
ومصادرة ،البقية، لأن الاختلاف فيما بينها ليس حقيقياً، وإنما هو اختلاف ألفاظ، إذ مبناه قائم على الإجمال والتفصيل وليس على الاستقلال والتفرد كما هو واضح عند التحقيق.
الرأي في هذه الدراسة
أما ما تتبناه هذه الدراسة بصورة رئيسة فالتصنيف الثلاثي، المعتمد على الحس والعقل والخبر المعصوم أو الوحي، دون إقصاء لما عدا ذلك. فالحس والإلهام والرؤيا الصادقة مصادر ثانوية تأخذ دورها بحسبه في هذه الدراسة. ألطف الاستشهادات التي تساق في معرض ترجيح هذا التصنيف ما استنتجه أحد الباحثين من قول الله تعالى: ﴿وَاللهُ أَخْرَجَكُمْ مِنْ بُطُونِ أُمَّهَاتِكُمْ لا تَعْلَمُونَ شَيْئاً وَجَعَلَ لَكُمُ السَّمْعَ وَالأَبْصارَ وَالْأَفْئِدَةَ لَعَلَّكُمْ تَشْكُرُونَ [النحل: ۷۸/۱٦] . فقد استنتج أن منافذ المعرفة ثلاثة هي:
1 - المعرفة (السمعية) وتدخل فيها العلوم النقلية ومنها علوم الدين، وإن نقلت عن طريق القلم والكتاب.
۲ - المعرفة (البصرية وتدخل فيها العلوم التجريبية، لأنها تقوم على الملاحظة والتجربة والقياس وأساسها البصر والمشاهدة.
- المعرفة (الفؤادية) أو (القلبية) وتدخل فيها المعرفة العقلية الخالصة، عن طريق عمليات العقل العليا من تأمل ونظر وتفكير واعتبار ... إلخ، بل إن الآية قد يمتد معناها ليشمل المعرفة (الروحية ويقصد بها المعارف التبعية من حدس وإلهام وسواهما (۱).
ويلاحظ هنا أنّ دخول العلوم النقلية في المعرفة (السمعية) وكذا التجريبية في (البصرية لا يتعارض وذلك القول الذي تبناه أحد الباحثين من قبل، وذهب فيه إلى أن المقصود بالسمع (السمعيات) في الآيات التي قرنت بين السمع والقلب والعقل، لأنّ ذلك اعتساف صريح لمدلول تلك الآيات، أما هذا القول المستشهد به هنا فله منطق يسوّغ إدخال علوم النقل ضمن مدلول (السمع) لا أنها لا تحمل سوى علوم النقل (السمعيات) فحسب.
بيد أن ما يورده بعضهم من اعتبار للكشف أو ما في حكمه، أو قريب منه مما لا ينضبط بقاعدة، مستبعد هنا جملة وتفصيلاً. وأحسن ما قد يقال فيه إنه أحوال خاصة قائمة على الرياضة والكسب والوجد والذوق والفناء، وتلك أحوال طائفة من الخلق لها من طقوس الفكر ، ومسالك التعبد، ما ينوء بالتزامها جمهرة الأمة وسوادها الأعظم.
ولما كان هدف هذه الدراسة متساوقاً وإفادة عموم الأمة - لا انتقاء طائفة من أبنائها لا تقف عند حد اعتبار الكشف مصدراً للمعرفة، بل تتعدى ذلك لتحصره فيه وحده، معتبرة الحس والعقل حجاباً يحول دون الوصول إلى المعرفة الحقة، وهذا ما عبر عنه أبو اليزيد البسطامي بقوله: ((أخذتم علمكم ميتاً عن ميت، وأخذنا علمنا من الحي الذي لا يموت)(۱) - لما كان ذلك هدف هذه الدراسة فإن أي خصوصيات تتعارض وهذا الهدف تعدّ مرفوضة غير مقبولة مهدرة غير معتبرة (1) . وفي ضوء هذا يمكننا القول بأن فلسفة التربية الإسلامية ترتكز في مصادرها وطرائقها الرئيسة على الحس والعقل والوحي، وهذا الأخير يتسم بالمرجعية المطلقة، في حين يتسم الأولان بالنسبية المحدودة بمعطيات الزمان والمكان. ثم تعتمد فلسفة التربية الإسلامية كلاً من الحدس والإلهام والرؤيا الصادقة مصادر ثانوية فرعية على النحو الذي سيرد تفصيله في المكان المناسب من هذه الدراسة بإذن الله - تعالى -.
Chapter Three - Introduction
And it is a petitio principii, the remainder, because the difference among them is not real, but rather it is a difference in words, since its basis is on conciseness and detail and not on independence and uniqueness, as is clear upon investigation.
The Opinion in This Study
As for what this study primarily adopts, it is the tripartite classification, based on sense, reason, and the infallible report or revelation, without excluding other than that. So sense, inspiration, and truthful dreams are secondary sources that take their role accordingly in this study. The subtlest citations brought in the context of preferring this classification is what one researcher concluded from the saying of Allah the Exalted: 'And Allah brought you out from the wombs of your mothers while you knew nothing, and made for you hearing, sight, and hearts that perhaps you would be grateful [Surah an-Nahl 16:78].' So he concluded that the avenues of knowledge are three:
1. Auditory knowledge, which includes transmitted sciences, including religious sciences, even if transmitted by pen and book.
2. Visual knowledge, which includes empirical sciences because they are based on observation, experiment, and measurement, and their basis is sight and witnessing.
3. Heart knowledge or cardiac knowledge, which includes pure rational knowledge, through the higher mental operations of contemplation, speculation, thinking, consideration, etc. Rather, the verse may extend its meaning to include spiritual knowledge, by which is meant subsidiary cognitions from intuition, inspiration, and others (1).
It is noted here that the inclusion of transmitted sciences in auditory knowledge and likewise empirical sciences in visual knowledge does not contradict that statement which was adopted by one researcher before, who went so far as to say that by 'hearing' in the verses that paired hearing with heart and mind is meant 'auditory evidence,' because that is a clear distortion of the denotation of those verses. But this statement cited here has logic that justifies including transmitted sciences within the denotation of 'hearing,' not that they carry only transmitted sciences (auditory evidence) alone.
However, what some mention of considering unveiling or what is like it, or close to it, that cannot be regulated by a rule, is excluded here completely and in detail. The best that may be said about it is that it is special conditions based on spiritual exercise, acquisition, ecstasy, taste, and annihilation, and those are conditions of a group of people who have their own rituals of thought and paths of worship, the commitment to which is burdensome for the majority of the nation and its great mass.
And since the goal of this study is consistent with benefiting the general nation - not selecting a group of its sons who do not stop at considering unveiling a source of knowledge, but rather go beyond that to restrict it to it alone, considering sense and reason a veil that prevents reaching true knowledge, and this is what Abu Yazid al-Bistami expressed by his saying: 'You took your knowledge dead from the dead, and we took our knowledge from the Living who does not die' (1) - since that is the goal of this study, any particularities that conflict with this goal are considered rejected, unacceptable, wasted, and disregarded (1). In light of this, we can say that the philosophy of Islamic education relies in its main sources and methods on sense, reason, and revelation, the latter being characterized by absolute authority, while the first two are characterized by limited relativity due to the givens of time and place. Then the philosophy of Islamic education adopts each of intuition, inspiration, and truthful dreams as subsidiary secondary sources in the manner that will be detailed in the appropriate place in this study, by the permission of Allah - the Exalted.
Source pages p. 208

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological introduction to the author's study, defining the classification of knowledge sources. The author begins by noting that differences among classifications are often verbal rather than real. He then states that his study adopts a tripartite classification: sense (al-hiss), reason (al-'aql), and infallible report/ revelation (al-khabar al-ma'sum / al-wahy). He acknowledges secondary sources like inspiration and true dreams. To support this classification, he cites a researcher's inference from Quran 16:78, where Allah mentions giving humans hearing (sam'), sight (basar), and hearts (af'idah). The researcher derived three corresponding knowledge types: auditory (sam'iyya) including transmitted sciences like religious knowledge; visual (bassariyya) including empirical sciences based on observation and experiment; and heart-based (fu'adiyya) including pure rational knowledge through higher thinking. The author defends including transmitted sciences under 'hearing' against a critic who limits sam' to 'auditory evidence' (sam'iyyat), arguing that the verse's context allows a broader interpretation. However, he explicitly rejects mystical unveiling (kashf) as a source of knowledge for the general public, calling it subjective and unregulated. He quotes Abu Yazid al-Bistami to show the problem of those who reject reason and sense in favor of direct mystical experience. Since his study aims to benefit the whole ummah, such exclusivist approaches are rejected. Finally, he summarizes that Islamic educational philosophy rests on sense, reason, and revelation, with revelation as absolute reference and the others as relative. Intuition, inspiration, and dreams are secondary. This passage establishes the epistemological foundation for the rest of the work.

Key Terms
الحس
العقل
الوحي
السمع
البصر
الفؤاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author's tripartite classification, which source of knowledge corresponds to the faculty of 'hearing' (al-sam')?
A) Rational knowledge and contemplation
B) Empirical sciences based on observation and experiment
C) Transmitted sciences including religious knowledge
D) Mystical unveiling and inspiration

Key Takeaway
The Quranic triad of hearing, sight, and hearts grounds three fundamental knowledge types: transmitted, empirical, and rational, forming the basis of Islamic epistemology.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Using the tripartite classification, categorize the following Islamic sciences: 1) Ilm al-Kalam (theology), 2) Ilm al-Tafsir (Quranic exegesis), 3) Ilm al-Tibb (medicine). For each, identify which of the three avenues (hearing, sight, heart) it primarily relies on and justify your answer from the passage.
?
The author mentions that a previous researcher restricted sam' to 'auditory evidences' (al-sam'iyyat). Explain why the author rejects this narrow interpretation and how the verse's context supports a broader inclusion of transmitted sciences.

Method of Learning

The Rejection of Mystical Unveilings as a Source for the General Public
Author rejects mystical unveiling as a knowledge source for the general public.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between primary and secondary knowledge sources and explain why the author rejects mystical unveiling (kashf) as a source of knowledge for the general public.

Core Idea
Mystical unveiling is subjective and unregulated; the ummah needs accessible, shareable sources.

In the second half of the passage, the author explicitly excludes mystical unveiling (kashf) from the primary sources. He argues that it is not reliably regulated and is only for a special few. Since his study aims to benefit the whole ummah, such subjective sources cannot be foundational. He even quotes Abu Yazid al-Bistami as an example of those who go too far by rejecting sense and reason entirely. This lesson will help you understand the author’s reasoning and the hierarchy he establishes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the source being rejected
The author mentions 'unveiling' (al-kashf) and similar subjective experiences (inspiration, ecstasy) that 'cannot be regulated by a rule.' He labels them as 'special conditions' for a select group.
These are distinct from the primary triad of sense, reason, revelation.
2
Find the reason for rejection
Kashf is not universally accessible or verifiable. The author says it is based on 'spiritual exercise, acquisition, ecstasy, taste, and annihilation' – practices that are 'burdensome for the majority of the nation.'
The study’s goal is to benefit the general ummah, not a particular group.
3
Analyze the quotation from Abu Yazid al-Bistami
Al-Bistami claimed: 'You took your knowledge dead from the dead, and we took our knowledge from the Living who does not die.' This attitude rejects sense and reason as veils. The author uses this as an example of a view that goes against the inclusive goal of the study.
The author does not dismiss al-Bistami entirely but shows that such exclusivity is incompatible with a broadly accessible epistemology.
4
Compare with the author’s own hierarchy
The author concludes that Islamic educational philosophy rests on sense, reason, and revelation as primary, with revelation having absolute authority. Intuition, inspiration, and truthful dreams are secondary, but kashf is excluded entirely for the general public.
Note the distinction: secondary sources are accepted in their place; kashf is rejected because of its unregulated nature and exclusivist claims.

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological introduction to the author's study, defining the classification of knowledge sources. The author begins by noting that differences among classifications are often verbal rather than real. He then states that his study adopts a tripartite classification: sense (al-hiss), reason (al-'aql), and infallible report/ revelation (al-khabar al-ma'sum / al-wahy). He acknowledges secondary sources like inspiration and true dreams. To support this classification, he cites a researcher's inference from Quran 16:78, where Allah mentions giving humans hearing (sam'), sight (basar), and hearts (af'idah). The researcher derived three corresponding knowledge types: auditory (sam'iyya) including transmitted sciences like religious knowledge; visual (bassariyya) including empirical sciences based on observation and experiment; and heart-based (fu'adiyya) including pure rational knowledge through higher thinking. The author defends including transmitted sciences under 'hearing' against a critic who limits sam' to 'auditory evidence' (sam'iyyat), arguing that the verse's context allows a broader interpretation. However, he explicitly rejects mystical unveiling (kashf) as a source of knowledge for the general public, calling it subjective and unregulated. He quotes Abu Yazid al-Bistami to show the problem of those who reject reason and sense in favor of direct mystical experience. Since his study aims to benefit the whole ummah, such exclusivist approaches are rejected. Finally, he summarizes that Islamic educational philosophy rests on sense, reason, and revelation, with revelation as absolute reference and the others as relative. Intuition, inspiration, and dreams are secondary. This passage establishes the epistemological foundation for the rest of the work.

What is the primary reason the author rejects mystical unveiling (kashf) as a source of knowledge?
A) It contradicts the Qur'an
B) It cannot be regulated by a rule and is only for a select group
C) It relies too much on sense perception
D) It was not mentioned by the Prophet

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the following claim: 'I have received a direct unveiling (kashf) that proves the existence of God without needing the Qur'an or reason.' How would the author of this passage respond? Explain using his criteria of regulation, universal benefit, and the hierarchy of sources.
?
The author accepts 'intuition, inspiration, and truthful dreams' as secondary sources. Why are these not rejected like kashf? What distinguishes them from kashf according to the passage?

Method of Learning
intermediate
5 min
Rejecting Exclusive Reliance on Mystical Unveiling (Kashf)
Author rejects extreme kashf-centered epistemology.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the epistemological error of making kashf the sole source of knowledge and explain why the study rejects it.

Core Idea
Knowledge cannot be limited to unveiling; sense and reason are essential gates to truth.

The author begins by distancing the study from an extreme Sufi position that claims unveiling (kashf) is the only valid source, dismissing sense and reason as obstacles. This sets the methodological foundation for an inclusive Islamic educational philosophy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the extreme position
Find the description of the group that confines knowledge to kashf alone and considers sense and reason as veils. The quote from al-Bistami illustrates this.
The quote: 'You take your knowledge dead from the dead...'
2
Understand the author's rejection
Note that any particularities that conflict with the aim of benefiting the general ummah are rejected. The author uses strong terms: marfuda ghayr maqbula, mahdara ghayr mu'tabara.
The rejection is absolute.
3
Distinguish the study's approach
Contrast the rejected view with the study's inclusive approach based on sense, reason, and revelation, which will be elaborated next.
This sets the stage for the positive methodology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولما كان هدف هذه الدراسة متساوقاً وإفادة عموم الأمة - لا انتقاء طائفة من أبنائها لا تقف عند حد اعتبار الكشف مصدراً للمعرفة، بل تتعدى ذلك لتحصره فيه وحده، معتبرة الحس والعقل حجاباً يحول دون الوصول إلى المعرفة الحقة، وهذا ما عبر عنه أبو اليزيد البسطامي بقوله: ((أخذتم علمكم ميتاً عن ميت، وأخذنا علمنا من الحي الذي لا يموت) - لما كان ذلك هدف هذه الدراسة فإن أي خصوصيات تتعارض وهذا الهدف تعدّ مرفوضة غير مقبولة مهدرة غير معتبرة. وفي ضوء هذا يمكننا القول بأن فلسفة التربية الإسلامية ترتكز في مصادرها وطرائقها الرئيسة على الحس والعقل والوحي، وهذا الأخير يتسم بالمرجعية المطلقة، في حين يتسم الأولان بالنسبية المحدودة بمعطيات الزمان والمكان. ثم تعتمد فلسفة التربية الإسلامية كلاً من الحدس والإلهام والرؤيا الصادقة مصادر ثانوية فرعية على النحو الذي سيرد تفصيله في المكان المناسب من هذه الدراسة بإذن الله - تعالى -.
وقبل البدء في تناول المصادر الرئيسة أشير إلى أن الابتداء بالحس فالعقل فالوحي هو ما تسير عليه هذه الدراسة وليس الابتداء بأصل المعرفة ومصدرها الرئيس وهو (الوحي) كما قد يُتوقع في دراسة تعتمد الوحي (القرآن) مادة لموضوعها. وأحسب أنّ لهذا المسلك ما يبرره إذ هو ينطلق من تصور مفاده أن السائد في الدراسات الوضعية المعنية بمصادر المعرفة وطرائقها إقصاء (الوحي)، وقد جارت هذه الدراسة تلك الدراسات من حيث الابتداء بالحس والعقل بوصفهما قاسماً مشتركا بين الجميع، لكنها فارقتها حين اعتمدت الوحي مصدراً وطريقة فتميزت وسيبرز هذا التميز في أثناء التناول. علاوة على هذا فإن بعض الأئمة لم يروا بأساً بتقديم الحس على الوحي والعقل كما فعل أبو المعين النسفي، أو تقديم الحس والعقل على الوحي، كما فعل ابن تيمية وذلك في معرض حديثهم عن مصادر المعرفة وطرائقها.
وأما تقديم الحس على العقل فقد انطلق من فرضية فحواها أن الحس شرط لازب لعمل العقل، ومن دون معطيات الحس المعرفية فلن يتمكن العقل من تقديم معارفه العليا.
And since the aim of this study is consistent with benefiting the general ummah—not selecting a group from its sons who do not stop at considering unveiling (kashf) as a source of knowledge, but rather go beyond that to confine knowledge to it alone, considering sense and reason as a veil preventing access to true knowledge—and this is what Abu al-Yazid al-Bistami expressed by his saying: 'You take your knowledge dead from the dead, and we take our knowledge from the Living who does not die'—since that is the aim of this study, any particularities that conflict with this aim are considered rejected, unacceptable, nullified, and not taken into account. In light of this, we can say that the philosophy of Islamic education is based in its main sources and methods on sense, reason, and revelation, and the latter is characterized by absolute authority, while the first two are characterized by limited relativity conditioned by the circumstances of time and place. Then the philosophy of Islamic education adopts intuition, inspiration, and true dreams as secondary subsidiary sources in the manner that will be detailed in its proper place in this study, by the permission of Allah—the Exalted.
And before beginning to discuss the main sources, I point out that beginning with sense, then reason, then revelation is what this study follows, not beginning with the origin of knowledge and its main source, which is revelation, as might be expected in a study that takes revelation (the Quran) as the material for its subject. I think this approach has its justification, as it proceeds from a conception that the prevalent in modern studies concerned with the sources of knowledge and their methods is the exclusion of revelation. This study has followed those studies in terms of beginning with sense and reason as a common ground among all, but it differs from them when it adopts revelation as a source and method, thus distinguishing itself, and this distinction will emerge during the treatment. Moreover, some imams saw no harm in giving precedence to sense over revelation and reason, as Abu al-Mu'in al-Nasafi did, or giving precedence to sense and reason over revelation, as Ibn Taymiyya did, in the context of their discussion of the sources of knowledge and their methods.
As for giving precedence to sense over reason, it proceeded from the hypothesis that sense is an indispensable condition for the operation of reason, and without the cognitive data of sense, reason cannot produce its higher knowledge.
Source pages p. 209

Commentary
This passage is a methodological preface explaining the order of sources of knowledge in the study. The author first clarifies the rejection of an approach that makes mystical unveiling (kashf) the sole source of knowledge, which considers sense and reason as obstacles. He quotes Abu al-Yazid al-Bistami to illustrate this extreme view, then asserts that such particularities are rejected. The study instead bases itself on sense, reason, and revelation, with revelation having absolute authority while sense and reason are relative. It also acknowledges secondary sources like intuition and inspiration. The author then explains why the study begins with sense, then reason, then revelation, rather than starting with revelation as might be expected. This is to engage with modern secular studies that exclude revelation; by starting with common ground (sense and reason) and then introducing revelation, the study distinguishes itself. The author cites support from scholars like Abu al-Mu'in al-Nasafi and Ibn Taymiyya, who also gave precedence to sense and reason over revelation in discussions of knowledge sources. Finally, the author explains that sense is a prerequisite for reason, as reason cannot operate without sensory data. This reasoning is analogous to the Islamic principle that knowledge of the world (via sense) leads to knowledge of the Creator (via reason), then revelation provides detailed guidance.

Key Terms
الكشف
الحس
العقل
الوحي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the author say about any particularities that conflict with the aim of benefiting the general ummah?
A) They are accepted if they come from a scholar.
B) They are rejected and considered not taken into account.
C) They are considered secondary.
D) They are ignored.

Key Takeaway
The study rejects any epistemological exclusivism that denies the role of sense and reason, especially when it contradicts the universal benefit of the ummah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Find a contemporary Muslim thinker who claims that direct spiritual experience overrides scholarly consensus. Using the author's criteria, evaluate whether that claim is acceptable.
?
Quote al-Bistami's statement and explain why the author rejects its implication for the study's methodology.

The Three Main Sources of Knowledge: Sense, Reason, Revelation
Author defines main sources and their authority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the roles of sense, reason, and revelation as main sources, and recognize the absolute authority of revelation.

Core Idea
Revelation is absolute; sense and reason are relative but necessary.

The author lays out the foundation of Islamic educational philosophy: sense, reason, and revelation. Revelation is given absolute authority, while sense and reason are limited by time and place. Secondary sources like intuition are also mentioned.

Guided Path
1
List the three main sources
From the passage, identify al-ḥiss (sense), al-‘aql (reason), and al-waḥy (revelation) as the primary sources. The author uses the phrase 'الحس والعقل والوحي'.
These are the pillars of the study's epistemology.
2
Understand the hierarchy
Note that revelation is characterized by absolute authority (al-marji‘iyya al-muṭlaqa), while sense and reason are relative (al-nisbiyya al-maḥdūda), conditioned by time and place.
This hierarchy guides all further discussion.
3
Recognize secondary sources
Intuition (al-hads), inspiration (al-ilhām), and true dreams (al-ru’yā al-ṣādiqa) are subsidiary sources that will be detailed later.
They are not primary but complementary.

Method of Learning

The Methodological Order: Why Sense and Reason Come First
Author justifies why the study orders sense, reason, then revelation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the study begins with sense and reason rather than revelation, and cite scholarly precedents for this approach.

Core Idea
Starting with sense and reason is a strategic choice to engage with secular epistemology, not a demotion of revelation.

The author anticipates a question: why not begin with revelation? He explains that modern studies exclude revelation, so starting with common ground (sense and reason) allows the study to then introduce revelation as a distinguishing element. He cites al-Nasafi and Ibn Taymiyya as precedents, and also gives a logical reason: sense is a prerequisite for reason.

Guided Path
1
Identify the expected order
One might expect a study based on the Quran to start with revelation, but the study does not. The author says: 'ليس الابتداء بأصل المعرفة ومصدرها الرئيس وهو (الوحي) كما قد يُتوقع'.
This deliberate choice is explained next.
2
Understand the strategic reason
The study follows modern studies in starting with sense and reason as common ground, then distinguishes itself by including revelation. The verb 'jārat' indicates following, then 'fāraqat' indicates distinction.
This approach builds a bridge to secular thought.
3
Recognize scholarly support
Al-Nasafi and Ibn Taymiyya also gave precedence to sense and reason in discussions of knowledge sources, validating the approach.
This shows the approach is rooted in Islamic tradition.
4
Comprehend the sense prerequisite
Sense is a necessary condition for reason; without sensory data, reason cannot produce higher knowledge. This is the logical justification for giving sense priority over reason.
The author states: 'الحس شرط لازب لعمل العقل'.

The Methodological Order of Sense, Reason, Revelation
Author explains his method: sense→reason→revelation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the methodological order of sources of knowledge in this study and explain the strategic reasoning behind beginning with sense and reason rather than revelation.

Core Idea
The study begins with sense and reason to engage all audiences, then elevates revelation as distinct.

This passage is a methodological introduction. The author explains why he starts with sense perception, then reason, and finally revelation, even though the study is based on the Quran. He aims to establish common ground with non-revelatory studies and later highlight the unique contribution of revelation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the author's claimed order of sources
In the first sentence, the author states: starting with sense, then reason, then revelation. Note that this differs from what might be expected (starting with revelation).
The order is: al-hiss → al-aql → al-wahy.
2
Recognize the author's justification for this order
The author claims positive studies exclude revelation, so by starting with sense and reason, this study engages common ground before introducing revelation as its distinguishing feature.
The author is not rejecting revelation; he is strategically building a bridge.
3
Examine the scholarly precedents cited
The author mentions Abu al-Mu'in al-Nasafi (who prioritized sense over revelation and reason) and Ibn Taymiyya (who prioritized sense and reason over revelation). This shows the author is within a tradition.
These examples strengthen the author's position by showing it is not novel.
4
Analyze the rationale for prioritizing sense over reason
The author states that sense is a necessary condition for reason: without sensory data, reason cannot operate. This is a foundational epistemological principle.
This mirrors the Aristotelian dictum that nothing is in the intellect without first being in the senses.

Before beginning to discuss the primary sources, I point out that starting with sense, then reason, then revelation is what this study follows, not starting with the origin of knowledge and its primary source, which is revelation, as might be expected in a study that takes revelation (the Quran) as material for its subject. I think this approach is justified because it proceeds from a conception that the prevalent in positive studies concerned with the sources and methods of knowledge is the exclusion of revelation. This study followed those studies in starting with sense and reason as a common denominator among all, but it departed from them when it adopted revelation as a source and method, so it became distinguished, and this distinction will emerge during the discussion. Moreover, some imams saw no harm in prioritizing sense over revelation and reason, as did Abu al-Mu'in al-Nasafi (508 AH), or prioritizing sense and reason over revelation, as did Ibn Taymiyya (d. 728 AH), in the context of their discussion of the sources and methods of knowledge. As for prioritizing sense over reason, it proceeded from the hypothesis that sense is a necessary condition for the operation of reason, and without the cognitive data of sense, reason cannot provide its higher knowledge.

Commentary
This passage is a methodological introduction explaining the author's chosen order of sources of knowledge: sense, then reason, then revelation. The author acknowledges that since the study is based on revelation (the Quran), one might expect to start with revelation. The author further explains that sense is a prerequisite for reason; without sensory data, reason cannot function. Critique of Dividing Senses into External and Internal
Critique of classifying senses into external and internal based on Quranic methodology.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the critique that the Quran does not classify senses into external and internal, and identify the verbal nature of disagreements among scholars on the inner senses.

Core Idea
The Quran does not divide senses; it highlights cognitive function, not classification.

In this passage, the author presents the traditional enumeration of inner senses from Islamic philosophy, then argues that the disagreement over their division is merely verbal. He then critically examines the claim that the Quranic mention of 'hearts' (af'ida) alongside hearing and sight supports considering intellect as an inner sense, showing that this interpretation lacks strong evidence and undermines the Quran's integrative methodology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the traditional inner senses enumerated
List the five inner senses mentioned: common sense (implied), imagination, illusion, memory, and imaginative faculty. Note their definitions from the Arabic.
The common sense is not explicitly listed but is the basis for imagination.
2
Determine the author's claim about the nature of disagreements
The author states that disagreements among scholars (like al-Baqillani) in the classification of inner senses are merely verbal, not about realities. Some combine, others separate.
This sets up the real disagreement that follows.
3
Locate the real disagreement
The real disagreement is whether the intellect ('aql) should be considered one of the inner senses, as the positivist empirical school holds.
The author introduces this to critique it.
4
Analyze the author's critique of the Quranic evidence
The author refutes those who use Quran 16:78 and 17:36 to support intellect as an inner sense. He argues that these verses do not classify 'fu'ad' as a sense; rather, they show integration of sensory and intellectual tools. The inference is a special one without strong evidence.
The author emphasizes that the Quran's method is to mention senses based on cognitive value, not composition.
5
Summarize the author's conclusion
The author concludes that the Quran does not divide senses into external and internal; it highlights their cognitive-perceptual value. The classification itself is neutral educationally; what matters is how senses are activated.
This ties back to the critique and its implications.

Chapter Three: First Section - The Sense
2- Imagination: meaning that the images that were imprinted in the common sense, when they disappear, can be remembered by returning to imagination. Therefore, imagination is the treasure of the common sense. 3- Illusion: meaning the power that perceives particular meanings, such as hostility which the sheep perceives from the wolf. 4- Memory: meaning that the passing of images of particular meanings in illusion can be remembered by returning to memory, therefore it is called the treasure of illusion. 5- Imaginative faculty: which relates some images to others, for example: the owner of this color has such a taste. (1) Upon reflection on the foregoing, it becomes evident to us that the disagreement in classification is a matter of difference in words, not realities, as some combine them and others separate them, including Imam al-Baqillani's naming of the inner sense as the sixth sense without restricting it to the inner senses. However, the real disagreement is in considering the intellect as one of the inner senses, as the positive empirical school held (2), even though some researchers in the framework of Islamic education agreed to that based on the saying of God Almighty: 'And Allah brought you out from the wombs of your mothers knowing nothing, and He made for you hearing, sight, and hearts that perhaps you would be grateful' [An-Nahl 16:78]. So he counted the hearts (af'ida) together with hearing and sight, considering them the most prominent blessings of the senses, singled out for mention over others, thus necessitating gratitude, and imposing human responsibility before the Creator, Glorified and Exalted: 'Indeed, the hearing, the sight, and the heart - all of those will be questioned' [Al-Isra 17:236]. The truth is that this is a special inference with no strong evidence to support it. The use of the verse of An-Nahl or Al-Isra as evidence can be general, encompassing the sources and tools of sensory knowledge represented by hearing and sight as an indication of the most important external senses, and it also includes the hearts (af'ida) or the heart (fu'ad) as a corresponding indication to the powers of intellect and its higher operations. Combining the sources and tools of knowledge - hearing, sight, and heart - solely under the category of senses - besides lacking strong support - disables a major signal from the signals of the manifestation of integration between sensory and intellectual sources and tools, which distinguishes the methodology of the Holy Quran in dealing with all phenomena in the visible world. It is known that the Holy Quran did not mention the intellect by its bare name or its root ('aql), but expressed it in verb form sometimes and with different names at other times. We will come to all of that in the detailed discussion of intellectual sources and methods, God willing. The conclusion regarding the classification of senses is that it was not the methodology of the Holy Quran to divide senses into external and internal, but it highlighted its methodology in mentioning senses on the basis of their cognitive-perceptual value, as it showed their function without speaking about their composition (1). From an educational perspective, this classification in itself has no negative or positive effects, because the ultimate outcome is only in activating the senses in the field of education in a way that clarifies their functions and achieves the purpose of their existence as reliable and essential sources and methods.
Source pages p. 215

Commentary
This passage is a critical discussion of the classification of the senses into external and internal, specifically addressing the inner senses as defined in Islamic philosophical tradition. The author begins by enumerating the traditional inner senses: imagination (al-khayal), which stores images from the common sense; illusion (al-wahm), which perceives particular meanings like the sheep's perception of hostility from the wolf; memory (al-hafidha), which retains meanings from illusion; and the imaginative faculty (al-mutakhayyila), which combines images. The author then notes that disagreements in classification are merely verbal, as some scholars combine or separate them differently, citing Imam al-Baqillani's term 'sixth sense.' The real disagreement, however, concerns whether the intellect ('aql) should be considered an inner sense, as the positivist empirical school claims. The author criticizes those who use Quranic verses—specifically 16:78 and 17:36—to support this, arguing that these verses do not equate the heart (fu'ad) with a sense but rather indicate integration between sensory and intellectual sources. The Quran never uses 'aql as a noun but as verbs or other terms, and does not divide senses into external/internal but focuses on their cognitive function. The author concludes that from an educational perspective, the classification itself is neutral; what matters is how senses are utilized in education to fulfill their purpose as means of knowledge.

The Four Empirical Foundations in Usul al-Fiqh
Author lists four principles from usul al-fiqh that form the basis of empirical research.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish the four usuli principles for establishing causal relationships.

Core Idea
These four principles operationalize causality for empirical inference in Islamic law.

This passage presents four principles from usul al-fiqh that together form a systematic method for discovering causes ('ilal) of rulings. Understanding them is essential for any jurist or researcher who wants to draw sound analogies and avoid spurious correlations.

Guided Path
1
Identify each principle's core operation
Read the Arabic passage and note the key phrase for each: itTirad (positive co-occurrence), in'ikas (negative co-occurrence), dawaran (both), and tanqih (refinement).
2
Classify positive vs negative correlation
itTirad and in'ikas are one-sided (ruling present when cause present; ruling absent when cause absent). Dawaran combines both; tanqih is an independent process.
3
Distinguish dawaran from itTirad and in'ikas
Dawaran requires variation in both presence and absence of the cause; itTirad only requires positive, in'ikas only negative. Dawaran is considered the strongest evidence.
The text says dawaran 'has been considered the very experiment' (عين التجربة).
4
Recognize tanqih as a two-step refinement
Tanqih involves first deleting unsuitable attributes (حذف), then specifying the cause among what remains (تعيين). This is not a correlational but a selective process.
Tanqih al-manat is specific to usul al-fiqh and not a general logical method.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهذه أسس هي: ١ - اطراد العلة: أي إنه كلما وجدت العلة في صورة من الصور وجـد الحكم على نحو مطّرد، وتلك قاعدة: دوران العلة مع الحكم وجوداً). ٢ - انعكاس العلّة: أي إنه كلما انتفت العلة انتفى الحكم، وتلك قاعدة: دوران العلة مع الحكم عدماً). ٣ - دوران العلة أي دورانها مع المعلول وجوداً وعدماً، وقد عُدّ هذا الدوران عين التجربة، بحيث يفيد القطع كلما كثر . ٤- تنقيح المناط: وهو عبارة عن عمليتين هما حذف مالا يصلح للعلية من أوصاف المحل، وتعيين العلة بين ما تبقى (الحذف والتعيين)(۳).
And these are the foundations: 1 - Consistency of the cause: meaning that whenever the cause is found in a form, the ruling is found consistently; that is the rule: co-occurrence of cause with ruling in existence. 2 - Concomitance of the cause: meaning that whenever the cause is absent, the ruling is absent; that is the rule: co-occurrence of cause with ruling in non-existence. 3 - Co-occurrence of the cause: that is, its co-occurrence with the effect in existence and non-existence; and this co-occurrence has been considered the very experiment, such that it yields certainty the more it recurs. 4 - Refinement of the ratio legis (tanqih al-manat): which consists of two operations: removing what is unsuitable for causation from the attributes of the subject, and specifying the cause among what remains (deletion and specification).
Source pages p. 219

Commentary
This passage enumerates four methodological principles derived from usul al-fiqh that underpin empirical investigation. The first is 'itTirad al-'illa' (consistency of cause): whenever the cause is present, the ruling is present. The second is 'in'ikas al-'illa' (concomitance): when the cause is absent, the ruling is absent. The third is 'dawaran al-'illa' (co-occurrence) of cause with effect in both existence and non-existence; this co-occurrence is considered the essence of experiment, and repeated observation yields certainty. The fourth is 'tanqih al-manat' (refinement of the ratio legis), which involves eliminating irrelevant attributes and identifying the precise cause—a two-step process of deletion and specification. These principles show how Islamic legal theory operationalizes causality for empirical inference.

Key Terms
اطراد العلة
انعكاس العلة
دوران العلة
تنقيح المناط

Key Takeaway

ItTirad al-'Illa: Positive Co-occurrence
Author defines itTirad as the first empirical foundation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define itTirad al-'illa and explain its role in establishing causation.

Core Idea
Positive co-occurrence between cause and ruling is the first pillar of empirical inference.

The principle of itTirad (consistency) is the most basic form of causal correlation: whenever the cause is present, the ruling is present. This lesson parses its definition and application.

Guided Path
1
Extract the definition from the text
Find the Arabic phrase for itTirad: 'كلما وجدت العلة في صورة من الصور وجد الحكم على نحو مطرد'. This tells you itTirad asserts a universal positive correlation.
2
Compare itTirad with in'ikas
ItTirad only covers presence; it does not guarantee that when the cause is absent, the ruling is absent. That's in'ikas. Understanding this distinction avoids overclaiming.
ItTirad alone cannot rule out other causes.
3
Identify the condition for itTirad to be valid
The text says 'على نحو مطرد' (consistently). This implies no counterexamples. If any case with the cause lacks the ruling, itTirad fails.
This is a testable condition: one counterexample disproves itTirad.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهذه أسس هي: ١ - اطراد العلة: أي إنه كلما وجدت العلة في صورة من الصور وجـد الحكم على نحو مطّرد، وتلك قاعدة: دوران العلة مع الحكم وجوداً). ٢ - انعكاس العلّة: أي إنه كلما انتفت العلة انتفى الحكم، وتلك قاعدة: دوران العلة مع الحكم عدماً). ٣ - دوران العلة أي دورانها مع المعلول وجوداً وعدماً، وقد عُدّ هذا الدوران عين التجربة، بحيث يفيد القطع كلما كثر . ٤- تنقيح المناط: وهو عبارة عن عمليتين هما حذف مالا يصلح للعلية من أوصاف المحل، وتعيين العلة بين ما تبقى (الحذف والتعيين)(۳).
And these are the foundations: 1 - Consistency of the cause: meaning that whenever the cause is found in a form, the ruling is found consistently; that is the rule: co-occurrence of cause with ruling in existence. 2 - Concomitance of the cause: meaning that whenever the cause is absent, the ruling is absent; that is the rule: co-occurrence of cause with ruling in non-existence. 3 - Co-occurrence of the cause: that is, its co-occurrence with the effect in existence and non-existence; and this co-occurrence has been considered the very experiment, such that it yields certainty the more it recurs. 4 - Refinement of the ratio legis (tanqih al-manat): which consists of two operations: removing what is unsuitable for causation from the attributes of the subject, and specifying the cause among what remains (deletion and specification).
Source pages p. 219

Commentary
This passage enumerates four methodological principles derived from usul al-fiqh that underpin empirical investigation. The first is 'itTirad al-'illa' (consistency of cause): whenever the cause is present, the ruling is present. The second is 'in'ikas al-'illa' (concomitance): when the cause is absent, the ruling is absent. The third is 'dawaran al-'illa' (co-occurrence) of cause with effect in both existence and non-existence; this co-occurrence is considered the essence of experiment, and repeated observation yields certainty. The fourth is 'tanqih al-manat' (refinement of the ratio legis), which involves eliminating irrelevant attributes and identifying the precise cause—a two-step process of deletion and specification. These principles show how Islamic legal theory operationalizes causality for empirical inference.

ItTirad is the positive test: cause present → ruling present.

In'ikas al-'Illa: Negative Co-occurrence
Author defines in'ikas as the second empirical foundation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define in'ikas al-'illa and explain its role in causal inference.

Core Idea
Negative co-occurrence confirms that the absence of cause implies absence of ruling.

In'ikas (concomitance) is the complement of itTirad: whenever the cause is absent, the ruling is absent. Together they form a stronger inductive argument.

Guided Path
1
Extract the definition from the text
Find the Arabic for in'ikas: 'كلما انتفت العلة انتفى الحكم'. This is a universal negative correlation.
2
Understand the relationship with itTirad
ItTirad covers positive presence; in'ikas covers absence. If both hold, you have a bi-directional correlation (which is dawaran). But in'ikas alone is one-directional.
In'ikas is necessary but not sufficient for absolute causal proof.
3
Test in'ikas with an example
Consider a proposed 'illa: 'intoxication' for prohibition of khamr. In'ikas would require that if something is not intoxicating, it is not prohibited (like grape juice). If there is a non-intoxicating drink that is prohibited, in'ikas fails.

Dawaran al-'Illa: Co-occurrence in Both Existence and Non-Existence
Author defines dawaran as the third and central empirical foundation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain dawaran al-'illa as the essence of experimental reasoning and its epistemic value.

Core Idea
Co-occurrence in both existence and non-existence yields certainty through repeated observation.

Dawaran is the combination of itTirad and in'ikas: the cause varies with the ruling in both directions. The passage calls it 'the very experiment' and attributes certainty to it with repetition.

Guided Path
1
Extract the definition from the text
Find the Arabic for dawaran: 'دوران العلة أي دورانها مع المعلول وجوداً وعدماً'. Note that it includes both positive and negative co-occurrence.
2
Understand the epistemic claim
The passage says 'وقد عُدّ هذا الدوران عين التجربة، بحيث يفيد القطع كلما كثر'. This means that repeated observation of co-occurrence yields certainty (qat').
This is a strong epistemic claim: multiple instances of concurrence produce certain knowledge.
3
Distinguish dawaran from the previous two
ItTirad and in'ikas are only one direction each; dawaran combines both and is considered the strongest empirical evidence in usul.
The phrase 'عين التجربة' (the very experiment) highlights its scientific nature.

Tanqih al-Manat: Refinement of the Ratio Legis
Author defines tanqih al-manat as the fourth foundation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to describe the two-step process of tanqih al-manat and apply it to a fiqh example.

Core Idea
Refinement of the ratio legis is the precise elimination and specification of the cause.

Tanqih al-manat is a unique usuli method that goes beyond correlation: it involves deleting irrelevant attributes and then pinpointing the effective cause among the remaining ones. This lesson dissects its two-step nature.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two steps in the text
The text says: 'حذف مالا يصلح للعلية من أوصاف المحل، وتعيين العلة بين ما تبقى'. First step: delete unsuitable attributes. Second step: specify the cause among what remains.
2
Distinguish tanqih from the correlational methods
Unlike itTirad/in'ikas/dawaran which observe correlations, tanqih is an analytical reduction that removes noise and identifies the precise 'illa.
Tanqih often follows the correlational methods to pinpoint the exact cause among multiple candidate attributes.
3
Apply tanqih to a simple fiqh example
Suppose the ruling is 'prohibition of khamr'. Candidate attributes: it is a liquid, it is intoxicating, it is made from grapes, it is fermented. By tanqih, we delete 'liquid' (not all liquids are prohibited) and 'made from grapes' (other intoxicants are prohibited), leaving 'intoxication' as the likely cause.

Islamic vs Western Empiricism: Data Domains and Limits
Comparison of Islamic and Western empirical methods and their data domains

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the data domains of Islamic empiricism (revealed) and Western empiricism (observational) and identify the limits of empirical method in religious sciences.

Core Idea
Shared method, different data: Islamic empiricism uses revelation; Western uses observation.

This passage traces the historical connection between Islamic and Western empirical methods, highlighting that while the logical structure is similar, the source of data differs. You will learn why an exaggerated separation between religious and natural sciences is misguided, and where the empirical method cannot reach.

Guided Path
1
Identify the historical claim
The author states that Western empiricism (John Stuart Mill, Roger Bacon, Francis Bacon) built upon foundations laid by Islamic science. Note the terminological differences but structural similarity.
2
Contrast the data sources
Religious sciences (fiqh, kalam) use revealed data: Quran, hadith, consensus. Natural sciences use observational data from nature. This is the key difference.
3
Evaluate the separation critique
The author argues that exaggerating the separation between the two sciences is ignorance of both. Understand why – they share the same inductive method but apply to different domains.
4
Recognize the limits of empiricism
Not all religious matters are open to empirical investigation. Unseen (ghaybi) and non-repeating historical events lie beyond the method's scope.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويلاحظ أن المنهج التجريبي الغربي عند (جون) استيورات مل) قام فيما بعد على مثل هذه الأسس، وإن اختلفت الاصطلاحات (۱). ثم تنبه (روجر بيكون) إلى أهمية العلم العربي فارتاده ونقل الكثير منه إلى إنجلترا وإكسفورد بالذات، كما نقل عدداً كبيراً من الكتب العربية المترجمة إلى اللاتينية. ونادى بأن معرفة العرب وعلمهم الطريق الوحيد للمعرفة الحقة لمعاصريه. ثم استلهم (فرانسيس بيكون منهج روجر، فكان أول تجريبي حقيقي في العصور الحديثة(٢). وإذا كان هناك ثمة فرق يذكر بين قواعد المنهج التجريبي عند المسلمين في علومهم الدينية وبين القواعد المستخدمة في العلوم الطبيعية فهو أن المعطيات المستخدمة في القياس الأصولي الخاص بالفقه والعلوم الدينية هي أحكام وحقائق دينية متمثلة في آيات قرآنية كريمة وأحاديث نبوية صحيحة وأحكام مجمع عليها، ومستنبطة منهما، في حين أن معطيات العلوم الطبيعية ليست سوى معلومات وحقائق طبيعية مستقرأة عن الطبيعة بالملاحظة والمشاهدة. وعلى ذلك فإن التفريق بين العلوم الدينية والعلوم الطبيعية على نحو مبالغ فيه يعد جهلاً بالاثنين معاً). ولكن ذلك لا يعني أن هذا المنهج يمكن أن يطال المباحث الدينية جميعها، كما هو الحال في بحث المسائل الطبيعية ذلك أن ثمة مناطق غيبية أو تاريخية لا تتكرار، فمثل هذه مما لا محال للمنهج التجريبي فيها، كما سيتضح ذلك أكثر فيما بعد.. بإذن الله - تعالى -.
And it is observed that the Western empirical method with (John Stuart Mill) later stood upon such foundations, although the terminologies differed. Then (Roger Bacon) became aware of the importance of Arab science, so he studied it and transferred much of it to England and especially to Oxford, and he also transferred a large number of Arabic books translated into Latin. He proclaimed that the knowledge of the Arabs and their science was the only path to true knowledge for his contemporaries. Then (Francis Bacon) drew inspiration from Roger's method, so he was the first true empiricist in modern times. And if there is any notable difference between the rules of the empirical method among Muslims in their religious sciences and the rules used in the natural sciences, it is that the data used in the usuli qiyas specific to fiqh and religious sciences are religious rulings and facts represented in noble Quranic verses, authentic prophetic hadiths, and consensus rulings, and derived from them, whereas the data of the natural sciences are only natural information and facts derived from nature through observation and inspection. Therefore, the exaggerated separation between religious sciences and natural sciences is ignorance of both. But that does not mean that this method can apply to all religious investigations, as is the case in researching natural matters, because there are unseen or historical areas that do not recur; such matters are beyond the empirical method, as will become clearer later, by Allah's permission.

Commentary
This passage traces the influence of Islamic empirical methodology on Western science through Roger Bacon and Francis Bacon. The author notes that John Stuart Mill's empirical method is based on the same principles but with different terminology. Then he addresses a key difference: Islamic empiricism in religious sciences uses revealed data (Quran, hadith, consensus), while natural sciences use observational data. He criticizes an exaggerated separation between them as ignorance of both. Finally, he acknowledges limits: the empirical method cannot apply to unseen or non-repeating historical matters, marking boundaries for the method.

Islamic vs Western Empiricism: Data and Scope
Comparison of Islamic and Western empiricism with data differentiation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the Islamic empirical method from Western empiricism in terms of data sources and scope of application.

Core Idea
Islamic empiricism uses divine revelation as data; the West uses nature alone.

The author compares the empirical methods of Islamic and Western thought. He notes that while the Western method of John Stuart Mill, Roger Bacon, and Francis Bacon later stood on similar foundations, the data used in each tradition differ fundamentally. This lesson unpacks that distinction and its implications for how we approach religious and natural sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Western empiricists mentioned
Find the names of the three Western thinkers the author references: John Stuart Mill, Roger Bacon, and Francis Bacon. Note the sequence: Mill's foundations, then Roger Bacon's reliance on Arab science, then Francis Bacon as the first true empiricist.
Roger Bacon transferred Arab science to Oxford.
2
Extract the key difference in data
Locate the sentence beginning with 'If there is any mentionable difference...' The author states that religious sciences use data from Quran, Hadith, and consensus (qiyas usuli), while natural sciences use empirical observations.
This is the central epistemological distinction.
3
Understand the warning against exaggeration
Read the line: 'exaggerating the distinction between religious sciences and natural sciences is ignorance of both.' The author is cautioning against a total separation—both use empirical methods, just with different data types.
Neither science is superior; both require evidence.
4
Note the limits of the empirical method in religion
Find where the author says the empirical method cannot apply to all religious matters, specifically unseen or historical events that do not recur. This sets boundaries for empirical inquiry in theology.
Examples: matters of the unseen (ghayb) are beyond empirical test.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الباب الثالث: الفصل الأول - الحس
ويلاحظ أن المنهج التجريبي الغربي عند (جون استيورات مل) قام فيما بعد على مثل هذه الأسس، وإن اختلفت الاصطلاحات (۱). ثم تنبه (روجر بيكون) إلى أهمية العلم العربي فارتاده ونقل الكثير منه إلى إنجلترا وإكسفورد بالذات، كما نقل عدداً كبيراً من الكتب العربية المترجمة إلى اللاتينية. ونادى بأن معرفة العرب وعلمهم الطريق الوحيد للمعرفة الحقة لمعاصريه. ثم استلهم (فرانسيس بيكون) منهج روجر، فكان أول تجريبي حقيقي في العصور الحديثة(٢).
وإذا كان هناك ثمة فرق يذكر بين قواعد المنهج التجريبي عند المسلمين في علومهم الدينية وبين القواعد المستخدمة في العلوم الطبيعية فهو أن المعطيات المستخدمة في القياس الأصولي الخاص بالفقه والعلوم الدينية هي أحكام وحقائق دينية متمثلة في آيات قرآنية كريمة وأحاديث نبوية صحيحة وأحكام مجمع عليها، ومستنبطة منهما، في حين أن معطيات العلوم الطبيعية ليست سوى معلومات وحقائق طبيعية مستقرأة عن الطبيعة بالملاحظة والمشاهدة. وعلى ذلك فإن التفريق بين العلوم الدينية والعلوم الطبيعية على نحو مبالغ فيه يعد جهلاً بالاثنين معاً). ولكن ذلك لا يعني أن هذا المنهج يمكن أن يطال المباحث الدينية جميعها، كما هو الحال في بحث المسائل الطبيعية ذلك أن ثمة مناطق غيبية أو تاريخية لا تتكرر، فمثل هذه مما لا محال للمنهج التجريبي فيها، كما سيتضح ذلك أكثر فيما بعد.. بإذن الله - تعالى -. ويسعى بعض الباحثين لتجلية الاتجاه التجريبي في القرآن الكريم من خلال النظر في القواعد التي يمكن إيجازها على النحو التالي:
١ - تسخير الله تعالى الكون للإنسان وثمة آيات كثيرة بهذا الخصوص لعل من أبرزها قوله تعالى: {اللهُ الَّذِي خَلَقَ السَّمَاوَاتِ وَالأَرْضَ وَأَنْزَلَ مِنَ السَّمَاءِ مَاءً فَأَخْرَجَ مِنَ الثَّمَرَاتِ رِزْقاً لَكُمْ وَسَخَّرَ لَكُمُ الْفُلْكَ لِتَجْرِيَ فِي الْبَحْرِ بِأَمْرِهِ وَسَخَّرَ لَكُمُ الأَنْهارَ، وَسَخَّرَ لَكُمُ الشَّمْسَ وَالْقَمَرَ الدَّائِبَيْنِ وَسَخَّرَ لَكُمُ اللَّيْلَ وَالنَّهارَ} [إبراهيم: ٣٢/١٤-٣٣].
٢ - العرض الواقعي لحال الكون حيث يعرض الكون أمام الإنسان في صورته المتحركة: {وَتَرَى الأَرْضَ هَامِدَةً فَإِذا أَنْزَلْنَا عَلَيْهَا الْمَاءَ اهْتَزَّتْ وَرَبَتْ وَأَنْبَتَتْ مِنْ كُلِّ زَوْجٍ بَهِيجٍ} [الحج: ٥/٢٢]. {وَالشَّمْسُ تَجْرِي لِمُسْتَقَرٍّ لَهَا ذَلِكَ تَقْدِيرُ الْعَزِيزِ الْعَلِيمِ، وَالْقَمَرَ قَدَّرْنَاهُ مَنازِلَ حَتَّى عَادَ كَالْعُرْجُونِ الْقَدِيمِ، لا الشَّمْسُ يَنْبَغِي لَهَا أَنْ تُدْرِكَ الْقَمَرَ وَلَا اللَّيْلُ سابِقُ النَّهارِ وَكُلٌّ فِي فَلَكٍ يَسْبَحُونَ} [يس: ٣٨/٣٦-٤٠].
٣ - فاعلية الإنسان أي إن الله تعالى دفع الإنسان لاستخدام حواسه وعقله من خلال الآيات المتحدثة عن الكون والمختتمة بمثل قوله تعالى: {وإن في ذلك لآيات لقوم يعقلون ... يتفكرون ... ويفقهون ...}، بل وتجعل آيــات القرآن الكريم المهمة العملية للإنسان هي القيام بدوره الفاعل في الكون والحياة: {قُلْ سِيرُوا فِي الأَرْضِ} [الأنعام: ١١/٦)، {فَامْشُوا فِي مَنَاكِبِهَا} [الملك: ١٥/٦٧]، {فَكُلُوا مِمّا رَزَقَكُمُ اللهُ حَلالاً طيباً} [النحل: ١١٤/١٢]، {وَهُوَ أَنْشَأَكُمْ مِنَ الْأَرْضِ وَاسْتَعْمَرَكُمْ فِيهَا} [هود: ٦١/١١].
(۱) هذه الآيات وإن كان ظاهرها الدعوة إلى استخدام العقل وعملياته العليا، ولكنها قبل أن تبلغ العقل لابد أن تمر بالحس.
Chapter Three: Section One - The Sense
It is noted that the Western empirical method (at John Stuart Mill) later stood on such foundations, although terminologies differed (1). Then Roger Bacon realized the importance of Arab science, he frequented it and transferred much of it to England and especially Oxford, as he transferred a large number of Arabic books translated into Latin. He proclaimed that knowing the Arabs and their science was the only path to true knowledge for his contemporaries. Then Francis Bacon drew inspiration from Roger's method, and he was the first true empiricist in modern times (2).
If there is any mentionable difference between the rules of the empirical method among Muslims in their religious sciences and the rules used in the natural sciences, it is that the data used in the fundamental analogy (qiyas usuli) specific to fiqh and religious sciences are rulings and religious truths represented in noble Quranic verses, authentic prophetic hadiths, and agreed-upon rulings, derived from them; whereas the data of the natural sciences are only information and natural facts inferred from nature through observation and inspection. Accordingly, exaggerating the distinction between religious sciences and natural sciences is ignorance of both. But that does not mean that this method can reach all religious investigations, as is the case in the study of natural matters, because there are unseen or historical areas that do not recur; such as these, the empirical method has no way into them, as will become clearer later, by the permission of Allah the Exalted. And some researchers seek to elucidate the empirical direction in the Noble Quran by considering the rules that can be summarized as follows:
1 - God's subjugation of the universe for man, and there are many verses about this, perhaps the most prominent being His saying: "Allah is He who created the heavens and the earth and sent down from the sky water, and brought forth from the fruits a provision for you; and He subjected for you the ships to sail through the sea by His command; and He subjected for you the rivers; and He subjected for you the sun and the moon, both constant; and He subjected for you the night and the day." [Ibrahim 14:32-33]
2 - The realistic presentation of the state of the universe, where the universe is presented before man in its moving image: "And you see the earth barren, but when We send down upon it water, it quivers and swells and grows of every delightful kind." [Al-Hajj 22:5] "And the sun runs on to its destination. That is the determination of the Exalted, the Knowing. And the moon, We have determined for it phases until it returns like an old palm stalk. It is not for the sun to overtake the moon, nor does the night outstrip the day. They all float in an orbit." [Ya Sin 36:38-40]
3 - Human effectiveness, i.e., that Allah the Exalted impelled man to use his senses and intellect through the verses speaking about the universe and ending with such as His saying: "Indeed in that are signs for a people who reason... who reflect... who understand..." Rather, the Noble Quran makes the practical task of man to be his active role in the universe and life: "Say, travel through the land" [Al-An'am 6:11], "and walk among its paths" [Al-Mulk 67:15], "and eat of what Allah has provided for you, lawful and good" [An-Nahl 16:114], "and He is the One who produced you from the earth and made you inhabit it" [Hud 11:61].
(1) These verses, although their apparent meaning calls for the use of the intellect and its higher operations, but before they reach the intellect, they must pass through the senses.
Source pages p. 220

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological discussion comparing the Islamic empirical method with Western empiricism and then outlining the Quranic foundations of the empirical approach. The author begins by noting that the Western empirical method of John Stuart Mill was built on similar foundations to the Islamic one, though terminologies differ. Then Roger Bacon recognized the importance of Arab science, transferred it to England and Oxford, and proclaimed that knowledge of Arabs is the only path to true knowledge. Francis Bacon then drew from Roger and became the first true empiricist in modern times. The author then contrasts the empirical method in Islamic religious sciences with natural sciences: in religious sciences, the data are divine rulings and truths from Quran and Hadith, used in 'qiyas usuli' (fundamental analogy); in natural sciences, data are empirical observations. Exaggerated separation between the two is ignorance. However, the empirical method cannot apply to all religious matters, such as unseen or historical events that do not recur. The author then explains that some researchers attempt to elucidate the empirical direction in the Quran through three rules: 1) God's subjugation of the universe for man, illustrated by a Quranic verse listing created and subjugated elements; 2) the realistic presentation of the universe in its dynamic state, with verses showing the transformation of barren earth and the regular motion of celestial bodies; 3) human effectiveness, where Quranic verses impel man to use his senses and intellect, and to actively travel and inhabit the earth. A footnote adds that these verses, though encouraging intellect, must first pass through the senses.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the main difference between the data used in the empirical method for Islamic religious sciences and natural sciences?
A) Religious sciences use empirical observations, while natural sciences use divine texts.
B) Religious sciences use divine rulings from Quran and Hadith, while natural sciences use empirical data from observation.
C) Both sciences use the same type of data, but with different methods.
D) Natural sciences rely on intuition, while religious sciences rely on experiment.

Key Takeaway
Islamic and Western empiricism share a method but differ in their data sources; religious sciences use revelation, natural sciences use observation, and neither should be dismissed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Compare the data sources in deriving a fiqh ruling (e.g., the obligation of prayer from Quran and Hadith) with a natural science claim (e.g., the boiling point of water from experiment). How does the method of proof differ, and what does this tell you about the unity of knowledge?

Method of Learning
intermediate
5 min
Three Quranic Foundations of Empiricism
Outlines three Quranic rules for empirical investigation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and explain the three Quranic rules (taskhir, 'ard waqi'i, fa'iliyyat al-insan) that form the foundation of the empirical method in Islam.

Core Idea
Quranic empiricism rests on taskhir, 'ard waqi'i, and fa'iliyyat al-insan.

The passage outlines three rules derived from the Quran that demonstrate an empirical orientation: God's subjugation of the universe (taskhir), the realistic presentation of nature ('ard waqi'i), and human effectiveness (fa'iliyyat al-insan). These rules encourage observation and active engagement with the world, forming a coherent method for knowledge acquisition.

Guided Path
1
Define al-taskhir (التسخير)
Find the first rule: God subjects the universe for man's benefit. The author cites verse 14:32-33 listing created and subjugated elements (ships, rivers, sun, moon, etc.). This establishes nature as a resource to be explored.
Taskhir implies nature is accessible and ordered for human use.
2
Define al-'ard al-waqi'i (العرض الواقعي)
Locate the second rule: the Quran presents the universe in its dynamic, observable state. Verses 22:5 (earth reviving after rain) and 36:38-40 (sun and moon running their courses) depict natural phenomena as they are—moving, changing, and observable.
This invites empirical study of nature's processes.
3
Define fa'iliyyat al-insan (فاعلية الإنسان)
Find the third rule: God impels humans to use their senses and intellect. The verses enjoin action: 'travel through the land,' 'walk among its paths,' 'eat of what Allah has provided.' The footnote adds that these calls to reason must first pass through the senses.
Human effectiveness combines observation and action.
4
Synthesize the three rules
Notice how the three rules form a coherent epistemology: God's subjugation makes nature knowable; the realistic presentation provides the data; human effectiveness supplies the active inquiry. Together they outline a complete empirical method.
The Quran thus encourages scientific investigation.
5
Reflect on the footnote's emphasis
Read the footnote: 'These verses, although calling for intellect, must first pass through the senses.' This underscores that empirical observation is the gateway to higher reasoning, a key principle of the empirical method.
Sensory data are foundational for rational thought.

(۱) هذه الآيات وإن كان ظاهرها الدعوة إلى استخدام العقل وعملياته العليا، ولكنها قبل أن تبلغ العقل لابد أن تمر بالحس.
Chapter Three: Section One - The Sense
It is noted that the Western empirical method (at John Stuart Mill) later stood on such foundations, although terminologies differed (1). Then Roger Bacon realized the importance of Arab science, he frequented it and transferred much of it to England and especially Oxford, as he transferred a large number of Arabic books translated into Latin. He proclaimed that knowing the Arabs and their science was the only path to true knowledge for his contemporaries. Then Francis Bacon drew inspiration from Roger's method, and he was the first true empiricist in modern times (2).
If there is any mentionable difference between the rules of the empirical method among Muslims in their religious sciences and the rules used in the natural sciences, it is that the data used in the fundamental analogy (qiyas usuli) specific to fiqh and religious sciences are rulings and religious truths represented in noble Quranic verses, authentic prophetic hadiths, and agreed-upon rulings, derived from them; whereas the data of the natural sciences are only information and natural facts inferred from nature through observation and inspection. Accordingly, exaggerating the distinction between religious sciences and natural sciences is ignorance of both. But that does not mean that this method can reach all religious investigations, as is the case in the study of natural matters, because there are unseen or historical areas that do not recur; such as these, the empirical method has no way into them, as will become clearer later, by the permission of Allah the Exalted. And some researchers seek to elucidate the empirical direction in the Noble Quran by considering the rules that can be summarized as follows:
1 - God's subjugation of the universe for man, and there are many verses about this, perhaps the most prominent being His saying: "Allah is He who created the heavens and the earth and sent down from the sky water, and brought forth from the fruits a provision for you; and He subjected for you the ships to sail through the sea by His command; and He subjected for you the rivers; and He subjected for you the sun and the moon, both constant; and He subjected for you the night and the day." [Ibrahim 14:32-33]
2 - The realistic presentation of the state of the universe, where the universe is presented before man in its moving image: "And you see the earth barren, but when We send down upon it water, it quivers and swells and grows of every delightful kind." [Al-Hajj 22:5] "And the sun runs on to its destination. That is the determination of the Exalted, the Knowing. And the moon, We have determined for it phases until it returns like an old palm stalk. It is not for the sun to overtake the moon, nor does the night outstrip the day. They all float in an orbit." [Ya Sin 36:38-40]
3 - Human effectiveness, i.e., that Allah the Exalted impelled man to use his senses and intellect through the verses speaking about the universe and ending with such as His saying: "Indeed in that are signs for a people who reason... who reflect... who understand..." Rather, the Noble Quran makes the practical task of man to be his active role in the universe and life: "Say, travel through the land" [Al-An'am 6:11], "and walk among its paths" [Al-Mulk 67:15], "and eat of what Allah has provided for you, lawful and good" [An-Nahl 16:114], "and He is the One who produced you from the earth and made you inhabit it" [Hud 11:61].
(1) These verses, although their apparent meaning calls for the use of the intellect and its higher operations, but before they reach the intellect, they must pass through the senses.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT one of the three Quranic rules for the empirical method?
A) Taskhir: God's subjugation of the universe for man
B) 'Ard waqi'i: The realistic presentation of the universe
C) Fa'iliyyat al-insan: Human effectiveness
D) Qiyas: Analogy as the sole method

Key Takeaway
The Quran establishes three empirical foundations: God's subjugation of nature, its realistic presentation, and the call for human active inquiry through senses and intellect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Find a Quranic verse not mentioned in the passage that exemplifies one of the three rules (taskhir, 'ard waqi'i, or fa'iliyyat al-insan). Identify which rule it fits and explain how it encourages empirical inquiry.

Method of Learning
intermediate
5 min
Quranic Call to Sensory Observation
Author discusses how the Quran uses sensory experience to promote empirical investigation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify three ways the Quran encourages sensory observation and explain how they lead to rational inference.

Core Idea
The Quran commands humans to observe the universe with their senses.

This lesson explores how the Quran uses vivid depictions of nature and human empowerment to stimulate sensory observation and rational reflection. The author presents three themes: God's subjugation of the cosmos for humans, the realistic portrayal of natural processes, and the call for humans to act effectively in the world.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three major themes in the passage.
Read the first section of the passage and list the three themes the author presents: (1) God's subjugation of the universe, (2) the realistic presentation of the universe in motion, (3) human effectiveness.
These themes are supported by specific Quranic verses.
2
For each theme, extract the key Quranic verse and note what kind of observation it encourages.
For theme 1, verse Ibrahim 14:32-33 lists natural phenomena subjected for man. For theme 2, verses Al-Hajj 22:5 and Ya-Sin 36:38-40 describe processes. For theme 3, verses like Al-An'am 6:11 and Hud 11:61 call for travel and settlement.
Observe how each verse either describes a fact or commands action that requires sensory engagement.
3
Explain how the verses link sensory observation to rational inference.
Many of these verses end with phrases like 'for a people who use reason' or 'who reflect.' This shows that observation is not an end but a means to understand and deduce deeper truths about God and the universe.
This is the core epistemological claim of the author.
4
Summarize the overall argument.
The author argues that the Quran actively promotes empirical investigation by providing both the motivation (God subdues nature) and the method (observe, travel, reflect) for humans to gain knowledge.
